Since the slope of the line is $\frac{2}{5},$ the line rises 2 units vertically for every 5 horizontal units.  Thus, a possible direction vector is $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = (0,0);
B = (5,0);
C = (5,2);

draw(A--B--C);
draw(A--C,red,Arrow(6));

label("$5$", (A + B)/2, S);
label("$2$", (B + C)/2, E);
[/asy]

This means any nonzero scalar multiple of $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ is a possible direction vector.  The possible options are then $\boxed{\text{B, E, G}}.$

A line with slope $\frac{2}{5}$ can have any direction vector that is a scalar multiple of $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix}$ or $\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}.$  In other words, we want the vectors that are proportional to these vectors.

The only possible vectors are $\boxed{\text{A},\text{B},\text{D},\text{E}}.$

\[\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ 4 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} + u \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 6 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -1 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} + s \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}\]for some $t,$ $s,$ and $u.$  This leads to the equations

3u - 2t - 2s &= -6, \\

Let
\[t = \frac{x - 2}{3} = \frac{y + 1}{4} = \frac{z - 2}{12}.\]Then $x = 3t + 2,$ $y = 4t - 1,$ and $z = 12t + 2.$  Substituting into $x - y + z = 5$ gives us
\[(3t + 2) - (4t - 1) + (12t + 2) = 5.\]Solving, we find $t = 0.$  Thus, $(x,y,z) = \boxed{(2,-1,2)}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Taking the given equations two at a time, we get
\begin{align*}
\frac{x - 2}{3} &= \frac{y + 1}{4}, \\
\frac{x - 2}{3} &= \frac{z - 2}{12}.
\end{align*}Equating these, we get
\[\frac{y + 1}{4} = \frac{z - 2}{12}.\]Hence, $y = \frac{5z - 14}{4}.$

Since $\frac{x - 2}{3} = \frac{y + 1}{4}$ and $x - y + z = 5,$
\[\frac{x - 2}{3} = \frac{\frac{5z - 14}{4} + 1}{4}.\]Solving for $x,$ we find $x = \frac{5z + 2}{3}.$

Then
\[\frac{5z + 2}{3} - \frac{5z - 14}{4} + z = 5.\]Solving, we find $z = 12,$ so $y = 7$ and $x = 22.$  Hence, the point of intersection is $\boxed{(22,7,12)}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
The line containing $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ can be parameterized by
\[\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a} + t (\mathbf{b} - \mathbf{a}) = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ must be equal to the angle between $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}.$  Thus,
\[\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{b}\| \|\mathbf{c}\|}.\]Then $\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{c}\|},$ so
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix} \right\|}.\]Hence,
\[\frac{13}{\sqrt{29}} = \frac{13 - 3t}{\sqrt{(-2 + 3t)^2 + (5 - 2t)^2}}.\]Then $13 \sqrt{13t^2 - 32t + 29} = (13 - 3t) \sqrt{29}.$  Squaring both sides, we get
\[169 (13t^2 - 32t + 29) = 29 (13 - 3t)^2.\]This simplifies to $1936t^2 - 3146t = 0,$ which factors as $242t(8t - 13) = 0.$  The root $t = 0$ corresponds to the vector $\mathbf{a},$ so $t = \frac{13}{8},$ and
\[\mathbf{c} = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3 \cdot \frac{13}{8} \\ 5 - 2 \cdot \frac{13}{8} \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 23/8 \\ 7/4 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $\mathbf{c} = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}.$  Then we want $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ and $\mathbf{c}$ to be linearly dependent, which means there exists a scalar $t$ such that
\[\mathbf{c} = t \mathbf{a} + (1 - t) \mathbf{b}.\]Thus,
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = t \begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} + (1 - t) \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -2t + 1 - t \\ 5t + 3(1 - t) \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 - 3t \\ 3t + 3 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $x = 1 - 3t$ and $y = 3t + 3.$

Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$
\[\frac{\mathbf{c} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{c}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|}.\]The second denominator is $\sqrt{10},$ and the second numerator is $\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} = 13,$ so
\[\frac{\|\mathbf{c}\|}{\sqrt{10}} \cos \frac{\theta}{2} = \frac{13}{10},\]where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}.$  Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ the angle between $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}$ is also $\frac{\theta}{2},$ which means $\cos \frac{\theta}{2} = \frac{\|\mathbf{b}\|}{\|\mathbf{c}\|}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\frac{\|\mathbf{c}\|}{\sqrt{10}} \cdot \frac{\|\mathbf{b}\|}{\|\mathbf{c}\|} &= \frac{\|\mathbf{b}\|}{\sqrt{10}} \cos \frac{\theta}{2} \\
&= \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 - 3t \\ 3t + 3 \end{pmatrix}}{\sqrt{10} \cdot \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1 - 3t \\ 3t + 3 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} \\
&= \frac{3t + 10}{\sqrt{(3t + 10)^2 + (3 - 3t)^2}} \\
&= \frac{13}{10}.
\end{align*}Hence, $(3t + 10)^2 = 169.$  We also want $t \neq 0,$ since otherwise $\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{b},$ which means $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ and $\mathbf{c}$ are not collinear.  We get $t = -\frac{13}{3},$ so
\[\mathbf{c} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} \frac{16}{3} \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Let the circumcenter $O$ of triangle $ABC$ be the origin.  Then
\[\overrightarrow{G} = \frac{\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}}{3}\]and $\overrightarrow{H} = \overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C},$ so
\[\overrightarrow{F} = \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}).\]Then
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \|\overrightarrow{A} - \overrightarrow{F}\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}) \right\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \frac{1}{3} \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{B} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{C} \right\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \|\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \cdot (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{A} + 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 4 \overrightarrow{C} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}  - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}), \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Thus, $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{3R^2}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
From the definition of centroid, $G = \frac{A + B + C}{3},$ so
\[3G = A + B + C.\]Thus, $\overrightarrow{GA} + \overrightarrow{GB} + \overrightarrow{GC} = \overrightarrow{0}.$

From the definition of orthocenter,
\[\overrightarrow{OH} = \overrightarrow{OA} + \overrightarrow{OB} + \overrightarrow{OC}.\]Then $\overrightarrow{GH} = 2 \overrightarrow{OF},$ so
\[\overrightarrow{OF} = \frac{\overrightarrow{OG} + \overrightarrow{OH}}{3}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\overrightarrow{AF} &= \overrightarrow{OF} - \overrightarrow{OA} \\
&= \frac{\overrightarrow{OG} + \overrightarrow{OH}}{3} - \overrightarrow{OA} \\
&= \frac{2 \overrightarrow{OG} - \overrightarrow{OA}}{3},
\end{align*}and similarly, $\overrightarrow{BF} = \frac{2 \overrightarrow{OG} - \overrightarrow{OB}}{3}$ and $\overrightarrow{CF} = \frac{2 \overrightarrow{OG} - \overrightarrow{OC}}{3}.$  Thus,
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \left\| \frac{2 \overrightarrow{OG} - \overrightarrow{OA}}{3} \right\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (4 \overrightarrow{OG} \cdot \overrightarrow{OG} - 4 \overrightarrow{OG} \cdot \overrightarrow{OA} + \overrightarrow{OA} \cdot \overrightarrow{OA}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (12 R^2 - 9 R^2 - a^2).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (12 R^2 - 9 R^2 - b^2), \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (12 R^2 - 9 R^2 - c^2).
\end{align*}Hence,
\[AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{8R^2 - \frac{1}{3} (a^2 + b^2 + c^2)}.\]

We want to find a linear transformation $T$ such that $T\begin{pmatrix}x\\y\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}0\\y\end{pmatrix}$ for all vectors $\begin{pmatrix}x\\y\end{pmatrix}$. This means that $T\begin{pmatrix}1\\0\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}0\\0\end{pmatrix}$ and $T\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}$. To find the matrix corresponding to $T$, we apply $T$ to the standard basis vectors and write the resulting vectors as columns of a matrix: $$T\begin{pmatrix}1\\0\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}0\\0\end{pmatrix}\quad\Longrightarrow\quad \begin{pmatrix}0\\0\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{0}&\mathbf{0}\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}1\\0\end{pmatrix}$$$$T\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}\quad\Longrightarrow\quad \begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{0}&\mathbf{1}\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\end{pmatrix}$$ Therefore, the matrix we seek is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix}0&0\\0&1\end{pmatrix}}$.

From the formula for a projection,
\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]This vector has magnitude
\[\left\| \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \cdot 2 = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2}.\]Thus, we want $\frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2} = \sqrt{3}.$  Equivalently, $|a \sqrt{3} + b| = 2 \sqrt{3},$ or $(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2 = 12.$

Also, $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(2 \sqrt{3} + 4b)^2 = 12.\]Then $2 \sqrt{3} + 4b = \pm 2 \sqrt{3}.$  This leads to the solutions $b = -\sqrt{3}$ and $b = 0,$ which in turn leads to the values $a = \boxed{-1}$ and $a = \boxed{2}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
The projection of $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}$ onto $\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ is
\[\frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4}\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus,
\[\frac{(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2}{16} + \frac{(a - b \sqrt{3})^2}{16} = 3.\]This simplifies to $a^2 + b^2 + ab \sqrt{3} = 12.$  Then $(a + b \sqrt{3})(a - b \sqrt{3}) = 12,$ or $a^2 - 3b^2 = 12.$

If $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ then
\[12 = a^2 - 3b^2 = (2 + b \sqrt{3})^2 - 3b^2 = 13 + b^2 (2 \sqrt{3} - 3).\]Hence,
\[b^2 = \frac{25}{6} - \frac{5 \sqrt{3}}{6}.\]We see that this is positive, so $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( 2 + \sqrt{3}, \sqrt{\frac{25}{6} - \frac{5 \sqrt{3}}{6}} \right)}$  is a solution.

From product-to-sum,
\[\sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ].\]Thus, we can make the sum telescope:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ &= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \frac{\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2 \sin 5^\circ} [(\cos 0^\circ - \cos 10^\circ) + (\cos 5^\circ - \cos 15^\circ) + (\cos 10^\circ - \cos 20^\circ) + \\
&\quad + \dots + (\cos 165^\circ - \cos 175^\circ) + (\cos 170^\circ - \cos 180^\circ)] \\
&= \frac{\cos 0^\circ + \cos 5^\circ - \cos 175^\circ - \cos 180^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 + 2 \cos 5^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then by the double-angle formulas,
\begin{align*}
\frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} &= \frac{1 + 2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ - 1}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 2.5^\circ}{\sin 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \cot 2.5^\circ \\
&= \tan 87.5^\circ.
\end{align*}Thus, $r = \boxed{87.5}.$

We have that
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin 5k^\circ &= \frac{\sin 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 10^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 15^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} + \dots + \frac{\sin 175^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 10^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 15^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} + \dots + \frac{\sin 175^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 5^\circ + \sin 10^\circ + \sin 15^\circ + \dots + \sin 175^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sum_{k=1}^{18} \sin (5k)^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}From the angle addition formula,
\begin{align*}
2 \sin a^\circ \cos b^\circ &= \sin (a^\circ + b^\circ) + \sin (a^\circ - b^\circ), \\
2 \cos a^\circ \sin b^\circ &= \sin (a^\circ + b^\circ) - \sin (a^\circ - b^\circ),
\end{align*}so
\[2 \sin a^\circ = \frac{\sin (a^\circ + b^\circ) + \sin (a^\circ - b^\circ)}{\cos b^\circ}.\]Taking $a = 5^\circ$ and $b = 5^\circ,$ we get
\[2 \sin 5^\circ = \frac{\sin 10^\circ + \sin 0^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ} = \frac{\sin 10^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin 5k^\circ &= \frac{\sum_{k=1}^{18} \sin (5k)^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 5^\circ \cdot \sum_{k=1}^{18} \cos (5(k - 1))^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} \\
&= 2 \sum_{k=1}^{18} \cos (5(k - 1))^\circ \\
&= 2 \left( \frac{1 - \cos 0^\circ}{2} + \frac{1 - \cos 5^\circ}{2} + \dots + \frac{1 - \cos 80^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= 18 \\
&= \tan 10.326^\circ.
\end{align*}Thus, $r = \boxed{10.326}.$

Let $\mathbf{a}$ be an arbitrary vector.  Let $\mathbf{p}$ be the projection of $\mathbf{a}$ onto $\mathbf{v},$ so $\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a},$ and let $\mathbf{r}$ be the reflection of $\mathbf{a}$ over $\mathbf{v},$ to $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{R} \mathbf{a}.$

Note that $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r}.$  We can use this to find the relationship between $\mathbf{R}$ and $\mathbf{P}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair D, P, R, V;

D = (3,2);
V = (1.5,2);
R = reflect((0,0),D)*(V);
P = (V + R)/2;

draw((-1,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-1)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--D,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--V,red,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--R,blue,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--P,green,Arrow(6));
draw(V--R,dashed);

label("$\mathbf{v}$", D, NE);
label("$\mathbf{p}$", P, S);
label("$\mathbf{a}$", V, N);
label("$\mathbf{r}$", R, SE);
[/asy]

Since $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r},$ $\mathbf{p} = \frac{\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{r}}{2},$ so
\[\mathbf{r} = 2 \mathbf{p} - \mathbf{a}.\]In other words,
\[\mathbf{R} \mathbf{a} = 2 \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a} - \mathbf{I} \mathbf{a}.\]Since this holds for all vectors $\mathbf{a},$
\[\mathbf{R} = 2 \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{I}.\]Thus, $(a,b) = \boxed{(2,-1)}.$

Let $\mathbf{u}$ be a vector orthogonal to $\mathbf{v}.$  Then
\[\mathbf{v} = \operatorname{proj}_{\mathbf{v}} \mathbf{u}.\]Also, $\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{v}$ is parallel to $\mathbf{v},$ so
\[\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{v} = t \mathbf{v}.\]Then $\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} + t \mathbf{v} = (1 + t) \mathbf{v}.$

Note that $\mathbf{I} - 2 \operatorname{proj}_{\mathbf{v}} = \mathbf{R}.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{R} \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{v}.\]Also,
\[\begin{aligned} (\mathbf{R} \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} &= [a \mathbf{P} \mathbf{u} + b \mathbf{I} \mathbf{u}] \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= [a \operatorname{proj}_{\mathbf{v}} \mathbf{u} + b \mathbf{u}] \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= (a \cdot 0 + b \cdot (1 + t) \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= b (1 + t) \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= b (1 + t) (\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v}) \\
&= b (1 + t) \|\mathbf{v}\|^2.\end{aligned}\]On the other hand,
\[\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{R} \mathbf{u} = 2 \operatorname{proj}_{\mathbf{v}} \mathbf{u} - \mathbf{u} = t \mathbf{v}.\]This implies that
\[\frac{\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{R} \mathbf{u}}{t} = \mathbf{v}.\]Hence,
\[(\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{R} \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \|\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{R} \mathbf{u}\| \cdot \|\mathbf{v}\|.\]We compute that
\[\begin{aligned} (\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{R} \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} &= (\mathbf{u} - a \mathbf{P} \mathbf{u} - b \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= (1 - a) \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v} - b \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= (1 - a) (1 + t) \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} - b (1 + t) \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
&= (1 - a - b (1 + t)) \|\mathbf{v}\|^2,\end{aligned}\]and
\[\|\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{R} \mathbf{u}\| = \|\mathbf{u} - a \mathbf{P} \mathbf{u} - b \mathbf{u}\| = \|(1 - a) \mathbf{v} - b \mathbf{u}\| = \sqrt{(1 - a)^2 + b^2 (1 + t)^2} \|\mathbf{v}\|.\]Thus, we want $(1 - a - b (1 + t)) \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 = \sqrt{(1 - a)^2 + b^2 (1 + t)^2} \|\mathbf{v}\|^2$ for all vectors $\mathbf{v}.$  Since $\mathbf{v}$ can be any nonzero vector, this reduces to $1 - a - b (1 + t) = \sqrt{(1 - a)^2 + b^2 (1 + t)^2}.$  Squaring both sides, we get
\[(1 - a - b (1 + t))^2 = (1 - a)^2 + b^2 (1 + t)^2.\]This simplifies to $3ab + b = 0.$  Since we want $b$ to be nonzero, $b = -\frac{1}{3}.$  Then $a = \frac{1}{3t}.$  Thus, $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( \frac{1}{3t}, -\frac{1}{3} \right)}.$

\begin{align*}

We have that
\[\cos 41^\circ + \sin 41^\circ = \sqrt{2} \sin A.\]Squaring both sides, we get
\[\cos^2 41^\circ + 2 \cos 41^\circ \sin 41^\circ + \sin^2 41^\circ = 2 \sin^2 A.\]Then $\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ = 2 \sin^2 A - 1,$ so
\[\sin^2 A = \frac{\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ + 1}{2}.\]Hence,
\[\sin A = \pm \sqrt{\frac{\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ + 1}{2}}.\]Since $0 < A < 90^\circ,$ $0 < \sin A \le 1,$ so
\[\sin A = \sqrt{\frac{\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ + 1}{2}}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\cos 41^\circ + \sin 41^\circ &= \sqrt{2} \sin A \\
&= \sqrt{2 (\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ + 1)},
\end{align*}so $\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ = 2 \cos^2 41^\circ - 2 \cos 41^\circ \sin 41^\circ - 1.$  Hence,
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 A &= \frac{\cos 2 \cdot 41^\circ + 1}{2} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 41^\circ - 2 \cos 41^\circ \sin 41^\circ}{2} \\
&= \cos^2 41^\circ - \cos 41^\circ \sin 41^\circ \\
&= \cos 41^\circ (\cos 41^\circ - \sin 41^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} (2 \cos 41^\circ \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} - 2 \sin 41^\circ \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{2} (\cos 82^\circ - \sin 82^\circ)}{4},
\end{align*}so
\[\sin A = \boxed{\frac{\cos 82^\circ - \sin 82^\circ}{2 \sqrt{2}}}.\]Note that we can remove the absolute value from $\sin A$ because $0 < A < 90^\circ.$

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/precalculus/answers/222_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Note that $\mathbf{D}$ and $\mathbf{R}$ are both $2 \times 2$ matrices.  By the uniqueness of the singular value decomposition, we can write
\[\mathbf{D} = \mathbf{U}_1 \begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_1^\mathsf{T}\]and
\[\mathbf{R} = \mathbf{U}_2 \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta & -\sin \theta \\ \sin \theta & \cos \theta \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_2^\mathsf{T},\]where $\mathbf{U}_1,$ $\mathbf{U}_2,$ $\mathbf{V}_1,$ and $\mathbf{V}_2$ are orthogonal matrices.  Then
\[\mathbf{D} \mathbf{R} = \mathbf{U}_1 \begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_1^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{U}_2 \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta & -\sin \theta \\ \sin \theta & \cos \theta \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_2^\mathsf{T}.\]Then $\begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_1^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{U}_2 \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta & -\sin \theta \\ \sin \theta & \cos \theta \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_2^\mathsf{T}$ is orthogonal.  Moreover,
\[\det \begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_1^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{U}_2 \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta & -\sin \theta \\ \sin \theta & \cos \theta \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{V}_2^\mathsf{T} = \det (\mathbf{D} \mathbf{R}) = -40,\]so $k = \pm 4.$

If $k = -4,$ then $\mathbf{D} = \begin{pmatrix} -4 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix},$ which is not possible, since $\det \mathbf{D} < 0.$  Therefore, $k = \boxed{4}.$

Applying the triple angle formula, we get
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= \left( \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x - \frac{1}{4} \sin 6x \right) \cos 6x + \left( \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x + \frac{1}{4} \cos 6x \right) \sin 6x \\
&= \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x.
\end{align*}Then by the angle addition formula,
\[\frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x = \frac{3}{4} \sin (2x + 6x) = \frac{3}{4} \sin 8x.\]Thus, $a + b = \frac{3}{4} + 8 = \boxed{\frac{35}{4}}.$

By sum-to-product,
\[\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x = \frac{1}{4} (\sin 2x + 3 \sin 6x - 3 \sin 2x - \sin 6x)(1 + \cos^2 2x - \cos^2 2x).\]Hence, the expression simplifies to
\[\frac{1}{8} (\sin 2x + 3 \sin 6x - 3 \sin 2x - \sin 6x)(2 \cos^2 2x).\]Since $\cos 2x = 1 - 2 \sin^2 x,$ $\cos^2 2x = (1 - 2 \sin^2 x)^2 = 1 - 4 \sin^2 x + 4 \sin^4 x,$ so
\[\begin{aligned} 2 \cos^2 2x &= 2 - 8 \sin^2 x + 8 \sin^4 x \\
&= 8 \cos^4 x - 8 \cos^2 x + 2. \end{aligned}\]Hence,
\[\frac{1}{8} (\sin 2x + 3 \sin 6x - 3 \sin 2x - \sin 6x)(2 \cos^2 2x) = \frac{1}{2} (\cos^4 x - \cos^2 x) (2 \cos^2 2x).\]Since $0 \le \cos^4 x, \cos^2 x \le 1,$ the function $f(t) = t^4 - t^2$ is maximized at $t = 1$ and minimized at $t = -1/\sqrt{2}$ and $t = 1/\sqrt{2}.$  Thus, $f(t)$ is at most 0.4375 in absolute value.

Therefore,
\[\left| \frac{1}{2} (\cos^4 x - \cos^2 x) (2 \cos^2 2x) \right| \le \frac{1}{2} \cdot 0.4375 \cdot 2 = \frac{7}{16},\]so $a + b = \boxed{\frac{23}{16}}.$

By the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[\cos A = \frac{10^2 + 10^2 - 12^2}{2 \cdot 10 \cdot 10} = \frac{7}{25}.\]Let $x = AD = DE = CE.$

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E;
real x = 250/39;

A = (0,8);
B = (-6,0);
C = (6,0);

E = interp(A,C,(10 - x)/10);

[/asy]

\[x^2 = x^2 + (10 - x)^2 - 2x(10 - x) \cos A = x^2 + (10 - x)^2 - 2x(10 - x) \cdot \frac{7}{25}.\]Then
\[(10 - x)^2 - 2x(10 - x) \cdot \frac{7}{25} = 0.\]Since $x \neq 10,$ we can divide both sides by $10 - x,$ to get
\[10 - x - 2x \cdot \frac{7}{25} = 0.\]Solving, we find $x = \boxed{\frac{250}{39}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $AD = DE = EC = x$. Since $\triangle ABC$ is isosceles with $AB=AC$, the median from $A$ is also an altitude.  Let this intersect $BC$ at $F$. We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find $BF$ and $CF$.
[asy]
pair A,B,C,D,EE,F;

A = (0,8.973);
B = (-6,0);
C = (6,0);
D = (-1.5,4.485);
EE = (1.5,4.485);
F= (0,0);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--F,red);
draw(D--A--EE,red);

label("$A$",A,N);
label("$B$",B,S);
label("$C$",C,S);
label("$D$",D,W);
label("$E$",EE,E);
label("$F$",F,S);
[/asy]

From the right triangle $ABF$, we know that $BF=\sqrt{AB^2 - \left(\frac{1}{2} BC\right)^2} = \sqrt{10^2-6^2}=8$.  Similarly, from the right triangle $AEC$, we know that $CE = \sqrt{10^2-\left(\frac{1}{2} BC\right)^2} = 8$.  Since $D$ and $E$ are each a distance of $x$ from $A$, we have that $BF - x = CD$ and $CE-x=BD$.  Adding these two equations gives us $2(8-x)=BC=12$, or $x = 2$.  Therefore, $AD = DE = EC = \boxed{2}$.

Expanding the determinant, we get
\begin{align*}
&\phantom{=}\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\ 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\ -g & -h & -i \end{vmatrix} \\
&= a \begin{vmatrix} 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\ -h & -i \end{vmatrix} - b \begin{vmatrix} 2d + 5g & 2f + 5i \\ -g & -i \end{vmatrix} + c \begin{vmatrix} 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h \\ -g & -h \end{vmatrix} \\
&\quad - g \begin{vmatrix} b & c \\ 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \end{vmatrix} + h \begin{vmatrix} a & c \\ 2d + 5g & 2f + 5i \end{vmatrix} - i \begin{vmatrix} a & b \\ 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h \end{vmatrix} \\
&= a [(-i) (2e + 5h) - (-h)(2f + 5i)] - b [(-i)(2d + 5g) - (-g)(2f + 5i)] + c [(-h)(2d + 5g) - (-g)(2e + 5h)] \\
&\quad - g [-(2f + 5i) c + (2e + 5h) b] + h [(2f + 5i) a - (2d + 5g) c] - i [-(2e + 5h) b + (2d + 5g) a] \\
&= -7i - 5ch - 5bg - 4cf - 2ah + 2di + 7gh + 7ei - 7af.
\end{align*}Now,
\begin{align*}
&a \begin{vmatrix} e & f \\ h & i \end{vmatrix} - b \begin{vmatrix} d & f \\ g & i \end{vmatrix} + c \begin{vmatrix} d & e \\ g & h \end{vmatrix} \\
&= a(ei - fh) - b(di - fg) + c(dh - eg) \\
&= aei - afh - bdi + bfg + cdh - ceg \\
&= -7 - 5c - 5b - 4f + 2d + 7g + 7e - 2a.
\end{align*}Hence,

From the angle addition formula, the numerator is
\begin{align*}
&(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) + (\cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ -  \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos (5^\circ + 20^\circ) + \cos (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ.
\end{align*}From the sum-to-product formula, $\cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ = 2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ.$

Similarly, the denominator is
\begin{align*}
&\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= (\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ +  \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin (5^\circ + 20^\circ) - \sin (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin 25^\circ - \sin 85^\circ \\
&= -2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ,
\end{align*}so the expression is equal to
\[\frac{2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ}{-2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ} = -\frac{\cos 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ} = -\sqrt{3} = \tan 120^\circ.\]Hence, the smallest such $\theta$ is $\boxed{120^\circ}.$

We can write the numerator as
\begin{align*}
&\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ \\
&= \cos (20^\circ - 5^\circ) + \cos (50^\circ - 35^\circ) - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ \\
&= \cos 15^\circ + \cos 15^\circ - (\cos 15^\circ - \cos 25^\circ) - (\cos 15^\circ - \cos 85^\circ) \\
&= 3 \cos 15^\circ - \cos 25^\circ - \cos 85^\circ.
\end{align*}
Also, the denominator can be written as
\begin{align*}
&\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ \\
&= (\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin 15^\circ - \sin 85^\circ - \sin 85^\circ - \sin 55^\circ \\
&= \sin 15^\circ - 2 \sin 85^\circ \cos 5^\circ.
\end{align*}
Hence, the fraction becomes
\[\frac{3 \cos 15^\circ - \cos 25^\circ - \cos 85^\circ}{\sin 15^\circ - 2 \sin 85^\circ \cos 5^\circ}.\]By the half-angle formula,
\[\cos 15^\circ = \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos 30^\circ}{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{6} + \sqrt{2}}{4},\]so
\begin{align*}
3 \cos 15^\circ - \cos 25^\circ - \cos 85^\circ &= 3 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{6} + \sqrt{2}}{4} - \cos 25^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \\
&= \frac{3 \sqrt{6} + 3 \sqrt{2}}{4} - (\cos 25^\circ - \sin 5^\circ) \\
&= \frac{3 \sqrt{6} + 3 \sqrt{2}}{4} - \sqrt{2} \cos 65^\circ - \sqrt{2} \sin 65^\circ \\
&= \frac{3 \sqrt{6} + 3 \sqrt{2}}{4} - \sqrt{2} (\cos 65^\circ + \sin 65^\circ) \\
&= \frac{3 \sqrt{6} + 3 \sqrt{2}}{4} - \sqrt{2} \sqrt{2} \cos 25^\circ \\
&= \frac{3 \sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{4} - \sqrt{2} \sin 65^\circ.
\end{align*}Also,
\begin{align*}
\sin 15^\circ - 2 \sin 85^\circ \cos 5^\circ &= \sin 15^\circ - (\sin 95^\circ - \sin 75^\circ) \\
&= \sin 15^\circ + \sin 75^\circ - \sin 95^\circ \\
&= 2 \sin 45^\circ \cos 30^\circ - \sin 95^\circ \\
&= \sqrt{2} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} - \cos 5^\circ \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{6}}{2} - \cos 5^\circ.
\end{align*}Therefore, the fraction simplifies to
\[\frac{\frac{3 \sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{4} - \sqrt{2} \sin 65^\circ}{\frac{\sqrt{6}}{2} - \cos 5^\circ}.\]We can ignore the factor of $\frac{1}{2},$ so we want
\[\frac{\frac{3 \sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{2} - 2 \sqrt{2} \sin 65^\circ}{\sqrt{6} - 2 \cos 5^\circ}.\]By symmetry,
\[-\cot (-\theta) = \frac{\cos \theta}{\sin (-\theta)} = \frac{\cos \theta}{-\sin \theta} = -\cot \theta,\]so
\begin{align*}
-\frac{\frac{3 \sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{2} - 2 \sqrt{2} \sin (-65^\circ)}{\sqrt{6} - 2 \cos (-5^\circ)} &= -\frac{\frac{3 \sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{2} + 2 \sqrt{2} \sin 65^\circ}{\sqrt{6} + 2 \cos 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{-3 \sqrt{6} + \sqrt{2} - 4 \sqrt{2} \sin 65^\circ}{2 \cos 5^\circ - \sqrt{6}}.
\end{align*}Since $\theta$ is acute and $\tan \theta = -\cot (-\theta),$ $\theta$ must lie in the third quadrant.  Therefore,
\[\theta = \boxed{195^\circ}.\]

We can write $\tan x = \sin x$ as $\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \sin x,$ so
\[\sin x = \cos x \sin x.\]Then $\sin x - \cos x \sin x = 0,$ or $\sin x (1 - \cos x) = 0.$  Thus, $\sin x = 0$ or $\cos x = 1.$

The solutions to $\sin x = 0$ are $x = 0,$ $\pi,$ and $2 \pi.$

The solution to $\cos x = 1$ is $x = 0.$

Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{0, \pi, 2 \pi}.$

From the identity $\sin^2 x + \cos^2 x = 1,$
\[\tan^2 x + 1 = 1,\]so $\tan^2 x = 0,$ which means $\tan x = 0.$

If $z^{13} = 1,$ then $z^{13} - 1 = 0,$ which factors as
\[(z - 1)(z^{12} + z^{11} + \dots + z + 1) = 0.\]If $z = 1,$ then $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} = 6.$

Otherwise, $z^{12} + z^{11} + \dots + z + 1 = 0.$  Let
\begin{align*}
a &= z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}, \\
b &= z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}.
\end{align*}Then
\[a + b = (z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}) + (z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}) = -1.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
ab &= (z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12})(z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}) \\
&= z^3 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{12} \\
&\quad + z^5 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{14} \\
&\quad + z^6 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{12} + z^{15} \\
&\quad + z^{11} + z^{14} + z^{15} + z^{16} + z^{17} + z^{20} \\
&\quad + z^{12} + z^{15} + z^{16} + z^{17} + z^{18} + z^{21} \\
&\quad + z^{14} + z^{17} + z^{18} + z^{19} + z^{20} + z^{23} \\
&= z^3 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{12} \\
&\quad + z^5 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z \\
&\quad + z^6 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{12} + z^2 \\
&\quad + z^{11} + z + z^2 + z^3 + z^4 + z^7 \\
&\quad + z^{12} + z^2 + z^3 + z^4 + z^5 + z^8 \\
&\quad + z + z^4 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^{10} \\
&= 3z + 3z^2 + 3z^3 + 3z^4 + 3z^5 + 3z^6 + 3z^7 + 3z^8 + 3z^9 + 3z^{10} + 3z^{11} + 3z^{12} \\
&= -3.
\end{align*}Then by Vieta's formulas, $a$ and $b$ are the roots of $w^2 + w - 3 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm \sqrt{13}}{2}.\]Hence, the possible values of $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}$ are 6, $\frac{-1 + \sqrt{13}}{2},$ and $\frac{-1 - \sqrt{13}}{2},$ so
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + w_3^2 = 6^2 + \left( \frac{-1 + \sqrt{13}}{2} \right)^2 + \left( \frac{-1 - \sqrt{13}}{2} \right)^2 = \boxed{43}.\]

We can write $z = \operatorname{cis} \theta,$ so $z^{13} = 1$ gives us $\theta = \frac{2 \pi}{13} k,$ where $k = 0, 1, 2, \dots, 12.$

Note that the powers of $z$ in $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}$ correspond to the binary representation of $13 - 1 = 1100_2.$  Therefore,
\[z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} = z^{1 \cdot 2^2} + z^{1 \cdot 2^3} + z^{0 \cdot 2^4} + z^{1 \cdot 2^9} + z^{0 \cdot 2^{10}} + z^{0 \cdot 2^{12}}.\]We can group the terms with the same exponent:
\begin{align*}
z^{1 \cdot 2^2} + z^{1 \cdot 2^3} &= z^4 + z^8, \\
z^{1 \cdot 2^9} &= z^{512}.
\end{align*}We can then write
\[z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} = z^4 + z^8 + z^{512}.\]To each nonnegative integer $n,$ we can associate a residue $r$ modulo 13 by division, so $n = 13q + r,$ where $q$ is a nonnegative integer and $0 \le r \le 12.$  There are thus 13 possible values of $z^n,$ modulo the solutions $z^0,$ $z^{13},$ $z^{26},$ $\dots,$ which are all equal to 1.  In other words, $|z^n| = 1$ if $n$ is a multiple of 13.

Therefore,
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 = (z^4 + z^8 + z^{512})^2 + (z^{12 - 4} + z^{12 - 8} + z^{12 - 512})^2 + \dots + (z^{12 \cdot 4} + z^{12 \cdot 8} + z^{12 \cdot 512})^2.\]We can group the terms of the form $z^{12k + 4},$ and write
\begin{align*}
&\quad (z^{12 \cdot 4} + z^{12 \cdot 8} + z^{12 \cdot 512})^2 + (z^{(12 + 1) \cdot 4} + z^{(12 + 1) \cdot 8} + z^{(12 + 1) \cdot 512})^2 \\
&= ((z^{13})^4 + (z^{13})^8 + (z^{13})^{512})^2 + ((z^{13})^4 z^4 + (z^{13})^8 z^8 + (z^{13})^{512} z^{512})^2 \\
&= (1 + 1 + 1)^2 + (1 + 1 + 1)^2 = 18.
\end{align*}We can group the terms of the form $z^{12k + 8}$ similarly, and obtain another contribution of 18.  We can pair up the terms of the form $z^{12k + 512}$ into nine groups:
\begin{align*}
&\quad (z^{12 \cdot 512} + z^{12 \cdot 512})^2 + (z^{(12 + 1) \cdot 512} + z^{(12 + 10) \cdot 512})^2 + (z^{(12 + 2) \cdot 512} + z^{(12 + 9) \cdot 512})^2 \\
&\quad + (z^{(12 + 3) \cdot 512} + z^{(12 + 8) \cdot 512})^2 + (z^{(12 + 4) \cdot 512} + z^{(12 + 7) \cdot 512})^2 + (z^{(12 + 5) \cdot 512} + z^{(12 + 6) \cdot 512})^2 \\
&= 2^2 + (z^{13})^{2048} + (z^{13})^{2048} + (z^{13})^{1536} + (z^{13})^{256} + (z^{13})^{1024} \\
&= 2^2 + 1 + 1 + 1 + 1 + 1 = 7.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\[w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \dots + w_k^2 = 2 \cdot 18 + 9 \cdot 7 = \boxed{72}.\]

By the scalar triple product, for any vectors $\mathbf{p},$ $\mathbf{q}$ and $\mathbf{r},$
\[\mathbf{p} \cdot (\mathbf{q} \times \mathbf{r}) = \mathbf{q} \cdot (\mathbf{r} \times \mathbf{p}) = \mathbf{r} \cdot (\mathbf{p} \times \mathbf{q}).\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 \\
&= \boxed{4}.
\end{align*}

From the vector triple product, $\mathbf{a} \times (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{c}.$  Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= \mathbf{a} \cdot [(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \mathbf{b}] \\
&= (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \\
&= 0.
\end{align*}Thus, the answer is $\boxed{0}.$

We use the observation that the first equation factors as a sum of squares of squares: \begin{align*}
(x^2 - y^2)^2 &= (x^4 - 2x^2y^2 + y^4) \\
&= (x^4 + 2x^2y^2 + y^4 - 4x^2y^2) \\
&= ((x^2 + y^2)^2 - 4x^2y^2) \\
&= (x^2 + 2xy - y^2) (x^2 - 2xy - y^2) \\
&= (x + y)^2 (x - y)^2.
\end{align*}Therefore $|x + y| = \sqrt[4]{8} = \sqrt2$ and $|x - y| = \sqrt2/\sqrt{2} = 1,$ so $x + y$ and $x - y$ are each equal to $1$ or $-1.$ Assuming $x + y = 1$ and $x - y = 1$ gives no real solutions, but assuming $x + y = -1$ and $x - y = -1$ gives possible solutions in $(x,y)$ of $(- 1 + \cos{\theta},- 1 - \cos{\theta})$ where $\cos{\theta}$ satisfies the equation \[(- 1 + \cos{\theta}) (- 1 - \cos{\theta}) = \frac{8}{4} = 2\] so $2 + \cos^2{\theta} = 0$ and $\cos{\theta} = \pm i\sqrt2.$ We discard the negative root, so $x = - 1 + \cos{\theta} = - 1 + i\sqrt2 \cos{\theta}$ where $\cos{\theta}$ is real. By $x^3 y - xy^3 = 2\sqrt3,$ \[( - 1 + i\sqrt2 \cos{\theta}) ( - 2i\cos{\theta}) (- 2 - i\sqrt2 \cos{\theta}) (2i\cos{\theta}) = 2^2 \cdot 3\]so the factorization becomes \[( - 1 + i\sqrt2 \cos{\theta}) ( - 2 - i\sqrt2 \cos{\theta}) = \frac{2}{3}.\]Square this and complete the square in $\cos{\theta}.$ This gives \[\cos^2{\theta} - \sqrt2 \cos{\theta} - \frac{1}{3} = 0.\]We discard the negative root, so $\cos{\theta} = \frac{\sqrt{23} + \sqrt6}{6}.$ Finally, $x = - 1 + i\sqrt2 \cos{\theta} = \boxed{\frac{4\sqrt6 - \sqrt{138}}{12}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
From the angle addition formula,
\begin{align*}
\sin \left( \theta + \frac{\pi}{4} \right) &= \sin \theta \cos \frac{\pi}{4} + \cos \theta \sin \frac{\pi}{4} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} (\sin \theta + \cos \theta).
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} (\sin \theta + \cos \theta) = \frac{1}{3}.\]Since $\cos \theta = \pm \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta},$

\[\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} (\sin \theta + \pm \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta}) = \frac{1}{3}.\]Squaring both sides, we get
\[\frac{1}{2} (\sin \theta \pm \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta})^2 = \frac{1}{9}.\]Then $(\sin \theta \pm \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta})^2 = \frac{2}{9},$ so
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 \theta \pm 2 \sin \theta \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta} + (1 - \sin^2 \theta) &= \frac{2}{9} \\
\pm 2 \sin \theta \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta} &= -\sin^2 \theta + \frac{7}{9} \\
\pm 4 \sin \theta \sqrt{1 - \sin^2 \theta} &= -4 \sin^2 \theta + \frac{28}{9}.
\end{align*}Square both sides again, to get
\[16 \sin^2 \theta (1 - \sin^2 \theta) = 16 \sin^4 \theta - \frac{224}{27} \sin^2 \theta + \frac{784}{81}.\]Let $y = \sin^2 \theta.$  Then $1 - \sin^2 \theta = \cos^2 \theta = 1 - \cos^2 \theta,$ so
\[16 y (1 - y) = 16 y^2 - \frac{224}{27} y + \frac{784}{81}.\]This simplifies to $81y^2 - 112y + 28 = 0,$ which factors as
\[(3y - 4)(27y - 7) = 0.\]Since $0 \le \theta \le \pi,$ $0 \le \sin^2 \theta \le 1,$ which means
\[y = \sin^2 \theta = \frac{4}{3} \quad \text{or} \quad y = \sin^2 \theta = \frac{7}{27}.\]Therefore, $\sin \theta = \pm \frac{2 \sqrt{3}}{3}$ or $\sin \theta = \pm \frac{\sqrt{7}}{3}.$

Note that $\sin 2 \theta = 2 \sin \theta \cos \theta.$

If $\sin \theta = \frac{2 \sqrt{3}}{3},$ then $\cos \theta = \pm \frac{1}{3}.$  This gives us $\sin 2 \theta = \pm \frac{4 \sqrt{3}}{9}.$

If $\sin \theta = -\frac{2 \sqrt{3}}{3},$ then $\cos \theta = \pm \frac{1}{3}.$  This gives us $\sin 2 \theta = \mp \frac{4 \sqrt{3}}{9}.$

If $\sin \theta = \frac{\sqrt{7}}{3},$ then $\cos \theta = \pm \frac{2 \sqrt{2}}{3}.$  This gives us $\sin 2 \theta = \pm \frac{4 \sqrt{14}}{9}.$

If $\sin \theta = -\frac{\sqrt{7}}{3},$ then $\cos \theta = \pm \frac{2 \sqrt{2}}{3}.$  This gives us $\sin 2 \theta = \mp \frac{4 \sqrt{14}}{9}.$

Therefore, the possible values of $\sin 2 \theta$ are $\boxed{\pm \frac{4 \sqrt{3}}{9}, \pm \frac{4 \sqrt{14}}{9}}.$

Let the triangle be $ABC,$ where $AB = AC.$  Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$  Let $H$ and $I$ denote the orthocenter and incenter, as usual.  Without loss of generality, we can assume that the inradius of triangle $ABC$ is 1.  As usual, let $a = BC,$ $b = AC,$ and $c = AB.$

[asy]
unitsize(8 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H, I;
real angleA = aCos(1/9);

B = (0,0);
C = (1,0);
A = extension(B, B + dir(90 - angleA/2), C, C + dir(90 + angleA/2));
D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2;
E = (B + reflect(A,C)*(B))/2;
F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2;
H = extension(B,E,C,F);
I = incenter(A,B,C);

draw(A--D,red);
draw(B--E,red);
draw(C--F,red);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(incircle(A,B,C));
draw(B--I);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill);
dot("$I$", I, dir(0));

label("$1$", (H + I)/2, E);
label("$1$", (D + I)/2, E);
label("$\frac{a}{2}$", (B + D)/2, S);
[/asy]

Since $\angle B = \angle C,$ $B = 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2}.$  Then from triangle $BDI,$
\[\tan \angle IBD = \frac{1}{a/2} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Since $\angle IBD = \frac{B}{2} = 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4},$
\[\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a}.\]From triangle $BDH,$
\[\tan \angle HBD = \frac{2}{a/2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From right triangle $ABE,$ $\angle ABE = 90^\circ - A.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\angle HBD &= \angle ABD - \angle ABE \\
&= B - (90^\circ - A) \\
&= A + B - 90^\circ \\
&= A + 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2} - 90^\circ \\
&= \frac{A}{2}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From the equation $\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a},$
\[\frac{\tan 45^\circ - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan 45^\circ \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a},\]or
\[\frac{1 - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Solving, we find
\[\tan \frac{A}{4} = \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}.\]Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \tan \frac{2A}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}}{1 - (\frac{a - 2}{a + 2})^2} = \frac{a^2 - 4}{4a}.\]But $\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a},$ so
\[\frac{a^2 - 4}{4a} = \frac{4}{a}.\]Then $a^2 - 4 = 16,$ so $a^2 = 20.$  It follows that $a = \sqrt{20} = 2 \sqrt{5}.$

Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{16}{8 \sqrt{5}} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}.\]Also, $BD = \frac{a}{2} = \sqrt{5},$ so from right triangle $ABD,$
\[AD = \frac{AB}{\tan \frac{A}{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{2/\sqrt{5}} = \frac{5}{2}.\]By Pythagoras on right triangle $ABD,$
\[AB = \sqrt{5 + \frac{25}{4}} = \frac{3 \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Finally, by the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$

Let the isosceles triangle have base $BC$ and let $D$ be the intersection of the altitudes.  Let the incircle touch $BC$ at $E$.  Let $F$ be the projection of $D$ onto $BC$, and let $X$ and $Y$ be the points of tangency of the incircle with $AB$ and $AC$, respectively, as shown below.

[asy]
size(150);
pair B=(0,0), C=(7,0), A=(3,4), D=foot(A,B,C), E=(1.8,0), F=(D.x,0);
pair X=foot(E,A,B), Y=foot(E,A,C);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(X--E--Y);
draw(D--F);
draw(incircle(A,B,C));
label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, NE);
label("$E$", E, S);
label("$F$", F, S);
label("$X$", X, NW);
label("$Y$", Y, NE);
[/asy]

Since $AD$ and $BE$ are altitudes, their product is equal to the area of the triangle:
\[AD\cdot BE = [ABC].\]  Since $ABC$ is isosceles, so are triangles $ABD$ and $ACD$, so
\[AD = CD = \frac12 BC.\]  In addition,
\[BE = EC = s - c,\]  where $s$ is the semiperimeter of the triangle and $c = AB = AC$.

It follows that
\[AD\cdot BE = \frac12 BC \cdot (s - c).\]  But $[ABC] = rs$, where $r$ is the inradius, so $AD\cdot BE = rs$.  Hence,
\[\frac12 BC \cdot (s - c) = rs,\] or
\[r = \frac{BC}{2(s - c)}.\]  Using the fact that the incircle is also the $A$-excircle of $\triangle AXY$, we can also write
\[r = s - AY.\]  Thus
\[s - AY = \frac{BC}{2(s - c)},\] which simplifies to
\[AY = s - \frac{BC}{2(s - c)}.\]

In triangle $ABC$, we have $AB = AC$, so $AX = AY$.  Thus $s - AB = AY$, so
\[s - AB = s - \frac{BC}{2(s - c)},\] which simplifies to
\[AB = \frac{BC}{2(s - c)}.\]  Therefore $\cos A = \frac{AB}{AC} = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}$.

By the Triangle Inequality,
\[\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| \le  \|\mathbf{v}_1\| + \|\mathbf{v}_2\| + \dots + \|\mathbf{v}_k\| = k.\]Then
\[k \ge \left\|\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{61} > \sqrt{49} = 7,\]so $k \ge 8.$

We can express $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ as the sum of 8 unit vectors, as shown below, so the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\boxed{8}.$

\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| &\ge \|\mathbf{v}_1\| - \|\mathbf{v}_2\| - \dots - \|\mathbf{v}_k\| \\

Extracting Answer from: 
From $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$
\[\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= 1 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2}. \quad (*)\]Also from $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$ we can take the dot product with $\mathbf{v},$ to get
\[\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{v} + (\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 1.\]By the scalar triple product, $(\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = 1 - \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}.\]From equation $(*),$
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= 1 - \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2.
\end{align*}Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{w}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{u}\|^2 \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \cos^2 \theta \\
&\le \frac{1}{2}.
\end{align*}Equality occurs when $\mathbf{u} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ and $\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1/2 \\ 1/2 \end{pmatrix},$ so the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

We have that
\[(\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{u} = 0.\]Expanding, we get
\[\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{u} + (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w}) \cdot \mathbf{u} = 0.\]Since $\|\mathbf{u}\| = 1,$ $\|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w}\| = \|\mathbf{w} \|.$  Also, $\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w}) \cdot \mathbf{u} = 0.\]Thus, $\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{u} = 0,$ so $\mathbf{w}$ is perpendicular to $\mathbf{u}$.

Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{v}.$  Then
\[|\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}| = |\mathbf{v}| = 1.\]Then $\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + |\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{u}|^2 = 1,$ which means $\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 = 1.$

Thus, $\mathbf{w}$ is a unit vector perpendicular to $\mathbf{u},$ so $\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w}$ is a unit vector.  By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} \le |\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}| = \|\mathbf{u}\| \|\mathbf{v}\| \sin \theta = \sin \theta.\]Equality occurs when $\mathbf{w}$ is a scalar multiple of $\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v},$ so its magnitude is 1.  In other words,
\[\mathbf{w} = \pm \frac{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}{\|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}\|}.\]Since $\mathbf{w}$ has to be a unit vector perpendicular to $\mathbf{u}$, we take
\[\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} \sin \theta \\ 0 \\ -\cos \theta \end{pmatrix}.\][asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair D, U, V, W;

D = (0,0);
U = dir(110);
V = dir(220);
W = rotate(90)*(-U[1],0,-U[3]);

draw(D--U,Arrow(6));
draw(D--V,Arrow(6));
draw(D--W,Arrow(6));
draw(rightanglemark(D,W,V,5));

label("$\mathbf{u}$", U, U);
label("$\mathbf{v}$", V, V);
label("$\mathbf{w}$", W, W);

dot("$\theta$", (-0.3,-0.2));
[/asy]

We can then compute that $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = \boxed{\sin \theta}.$

From the product-to-sum formula, $\cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ = \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ),$ so
\begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ &= \cos 10^\circ \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cos 10^\circ (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} (\cos 10^\circ \cos 120^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right).
\end{align*}Applying the product-to-sum formula again, we get
\begin{align*}
\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right) &= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \frac{\cos 30^\circ + \cos 10^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{8} \cos 30^\circ \\
&= \boxed{\frac{3}{16}}.
\end{align*}

Rotating 60 degrees clockwise is the same as rotating $-60^\circ$ counterclockwise.  Therefore, we can apply a $-60^\circ$ rotation to $3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$ to obtain the resulting complex number.  We start with the complex number
$$3 \sqrt{3} - 5i = 10 \left( \frac{3 \sqrt{3}}{10} - \frac{5i}{10} \right) = 10 \operatorname{cis}  \left( \arctan \frac{-5}{3 \sqrt{3}} \right),$$where $\operatorname{cis} \theta = \cos \theta + i \sin \theta.$  Applying a $-60^\circ$ rotation, we then multiply by $\operatorname{cis} (-60^\circ) = \cos (-60^\circ) + i \sin (-60^\circ) = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i:$
\begin{align*}
10 \operatorname{cis}  \left( \arctan \frac{-5}{3 \sqrt{3}} \right) \cdot \left( \frac{1}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) &= 5 \operatorname{cis} \left( \arctan \frac{-5}{3 \sqrt{3}} - 60^\circ \right) \\
&= 5 \operatorname{cis} \left( \arctan \frac{-5}{3 \sqrt{3}} - \arctan \sqrt{3} \right) \\
&= 5 \operatorname{cis} \left( \arctan \frac{\sqrt{3} - 5}{3 \sqrt{3}} \right) \\
&= \boxed{\frac{\sqrt{39} - 5 \sqrt{3}}{2} - \frac{5 + \sqrt{13}}{2} i}.
\end{align*}

\[\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} + t \left( \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right) = \begin{pmatrix} 3t \\ -2 + 2t \\ -5 + 4t \end{pmatrix}.\]When the line intersects the $xy$-plane, the $z$-coordinate is $0.$  Hence, $-5 + 4t = 0,$ so $t = \frac{5}{4}.$  Then the vector becomes

Note the similarity of the recursion to the angle addition identity
\[\tan (x + y) = \frac{\tan x + \tan y}{1 - \tan x \tan y}.\]We can take advantage of this similarity as follows: Let $f_1 = 3,$ $f_2 = 2,$ and let $f_n = f_{n - 1} + f_{n - 2}$ for all $n \ge 3.$  Let $\theta_n = \frac{f_n \pi}{12}.$  Then $\tan \theta_1 = \tan \frac{\pi}{4} = 1$ and $\tan \theta_2 = \tan \frac{\pi}{6} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Also,
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta_{n + 2} &= \tan (\theta_{n + 1} + \theta_n) \\
&= \frac{\tan \theta_{n + 1} + \tan \theta_n}{1 - \tan \theta_n \tan \theta_{n + 1}}.
\end{align*}Since the sequences $(a_n)$ and $(\tan \theta_n)$ have the same initial terms, and the same recursion, they coincide.

Since $\tan \theta$ is periodic with period $\pi,$ to compute further terms of $\tan \theta_n,$ it suffices to compute $f_n$ modulo 12:
\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
n & f_n \pmod{12} \\ \hline
1 & 3 \\
2 & 2 \\
3 & 5 \\
4 & 7 \\

8 & 2 \\

17 & 0 \\
18 & 11 \\
19 & 11 \\
20 & 10 \\
21 & 9 \\
22 & 7 \\
23 & 4 \\
24 & 11 \\
25 & 3 \\
26 & 2
\end{array}
\]Since $a_{25} \equiv a_1 \pmod{12}$ and $a_{26} \equiv a_2 \pmod{12},$ the sequence modulo 12 become periodic at this point, with period 12.

Therefore,

Let $b_n=\arcsin(a_n),$ where $-\frac {\pi}{2}<b_n<\frac {\pi}{2}.$ Then, \begin{align*} 2\sin b_{n+2} &= \frac {2\sin b_n \cos b_{n+1} + 2\cos b_n \sin b_{n+1}}{\cos b_nb_{n+1} - \sin b_n\sin b_{n+1}}\\ &=\frac{\sin{(b_n+b_{n+1})}}{\cos b_nb_{n+1} - \sin b_n\sin b_{n+1}}, \end{align*}by sum-to-product. Thus, \begin{align*} \sin(b_{n+2}+b_n+b_{n+1}) &= \sin b_{n+2}(\cos b_nb_{n+1} - \sin b_n\sin b_{n+1}) \\ &=\sin b_n \cos b_{n+1} + \cos b_n \sin b_{n+1} \\ &= \sin(b_n+b_{n+1}). \end{align*}So we see that $b_n+b_{n+1}+b_{n+2}$ is an integer multiple of $\pi$ for all $n$. Let $b_n+b_{n+1}+b_{n+2} = k_n\pi$. It follows that $b_{n-1}+b_n+b_{n+1}=(k_{n-1}+k_n)\pi$, and $b_{n-2}+b_{n-1}+b_n=(k_{n-2}+k_{n-1})\pi$. We will show that $k_n$ is constant. Squaring the equation $b_n+b_{n+1}+b_{n+2} = k_n\pi$, we have, after substituting $\sin b_n = a_n,$ etc, \begin{align*} &\qquad\cos b_nb_{n+2}-\cos^2b_n-\sin^2b_{n+2}=\sin b_n\sin b_{n+2}(\cos b_nb_{n+2}-1) \\ &\Rightarrow\quad\cos(b_{n+1}-b_{n-1})=\frac {a_n(1-a_{n+2}^2)-a_{n+2}(1-a_n^2)}{a_na_{n+2}(1-a_na_{n+2})}, \end{align*}which is only a function of $a_n,a_{n+2}.$ So $k_n$ is determined by $a_n,a_{n+2}$ only. It therefore follows that $k_n$ is constant, since the starting values $k_1$ and $k_2$ are integers (we are given $a_1$ and $a_2$), and $k_n\in\mathbb{Z}$ for all $n$. So we may assume $k_n$ to be a constant, which we will denote by $k$.

Hence $a_{n-1}+a_n+a_{n+1}=m\pi$ for some $m\in\mathbb{Z},$ which implies that $a_{n-1}a_n+a_{n-1}a_{n+1}+a_na_{n+1} = 1.$ Let $s_n = a_{n-1}+a_n.$ Then, $a_{n+1}=\frac {1-a_na_{n-1}}{1-{a_n}^2}=\frac {s_n-s_{n-1}a_n}{1-{a_n}^2}.$ So, we have \[\frac{s_n^2-s_n s_{n-1} a_n}{1-a_n^2}+s_n a_n+\frac {s_n s_{n-1}a_n-s_{n-1}^2a_n^2}{1-a_n^2}=1.\]Thus, $s_n^2-a_ns_n s_{n-1}+a_n^2 s_{n-1}^2=a_n^2-s_n^2.$ Since $a_1=1$ and $a_2=\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}},$ we have $s_1=1+\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}},$ $s_2=\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}}+a_2=\frac {2}{\sqrt {3}}.$ Also, note that $s_n$ is invertible as long as $|a_n|<1.$ Hence, \[a_n =\frac {s_{n-1}s_{n-2}-s_ns_{n-3}}{s_{n-1}^2-s_ns_{n-2}}\] for all $n\ge3,$ and we can compute the $a_i$ recursively. From $\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b} = \begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -7 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix},$ $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\| = \sqrt{6^2 + (-7)^2 + 3^2} = 2 \sqrt{14}.$  Then by the scalar triple product,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{b} \cdot (\mathbf{c} \times \mathbf{a}) = \mathbf{c} \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) = \begin{vmatrix} a_1 & a_2 & a_3 \\ b_1 & b_2 & b_3 \\ c_1 & c_2 & c_3 \end{vmatrix}.\]Since $\mathbf{b} \cdot (\mathbf{c} \times \mathbf{a}) = (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) \cdot \mathbf{a} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -7 \\ 18 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \mathbf{a} = 4 \cdot 1 + (-7) \cdot (-7) + 18 \cdot 3 = 81 = \begin{vmatrix} a_1 & a_2 & a_3 \\ b_1 & b_2 & b_3 \\ c_1 & c_2 & c_3 \end{vmatrix},$
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{b} \cdot (\mathbf{c} \times \mathbf{a}) = \mathbf{c} \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) = 81.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{c} \times (3 \mathbf{a} - 2 \mathbf{b})\| &= \|3 \mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{c} - 2 \mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}\| \\
&= 3 \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{c}\| - 2 \|\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}\| \\
&= 3 \cdot 3 \sqrt{3} - 2 \cdot \sqrt{354} \\
&= \sqrt{27} \cdot 7 - 2 \sqrt{354} \\
&= 5 \sqrt{27} - 2 \sqrt{354}.
\end{align*}Note that
\[\|\mathbf{c} \times (3 \mathbf{a} - 2 \mathbf{b})\|^2 = (5 \sqrt{27} - 2 \sqrt{354})^2 = 45 \cdot 5 - 4 \cdot 5 \cdot 6 \cdot \sqrt{66},\]and
\[\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b} + 2 \mathbf{c}\|^2 = (2 \sqrt{14} + 2 \sqrt{29})^2 = 56 + 116 + 4 \cdot 2 \sqrt{406}.\]Thus,
\[56 + 116 + 4 \cdot 2 \sqrt{406} = 225 - 120 \sqrt{11}.\]Solving, we find $\sqrt{406} = \frac{35 - 3 \sqrt{11}}{4},$ so
\[\|\mathbf{c} \times (3 \mathbf{a} - 2 \mathbf{b})\| = 5 \sqrt{27} - 2 \sqrt{354} = \sqrt{54} \cdot 5 - \sqrt{29} \cdot 7.\]Therefore, $k = 5 + 7 = \boxed{12}.$

We use the conversion formulas:

$x = r \cos \theta$
$y = r \sin \theta$
$z = h$

where $r$ is the radius, $\theta$ is the angle from the positive $x$-axis, and $h$ is the height or $z$-coordinate.

In cylindrical coordinates, we have $r = 6 \sqrt{3}$, $\theta = \frac{5\pi}{3}$, and $h = -2$. Note that $\frac{5\pi}{3}$ is in the fourth quadrant, so we need to add $2\pi$ to get an angle in the first quadrant.

$\theta' = \frac{5\pi}{3} + 2\pi = \frac{11\pi}{3}$

Now we can convert to rectangular coordinates:

$x = r \cos \theta' = (6 \sqrt{3}) \cos \frac{11\pi}{3} = (6 \sqrt{3}) \cos \frac{\pi}{3} = 9$

$y = r \sin \theta' = (6 \sqrt{3}) \sin \frac{11\pi}{3} = (6 \sqrt{3}) \sin \frac{\pi}{3} = 9$

$z = h = -2$

Therefore, the rectangular coordinates are $\boxed{(9, 9, -2)}$.

\[\mathbf{a} = t \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} t \\ 2t \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalar $t.$  Then
\[\mathbf{b} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} t \\ 2t \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 - t \\ 7 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]We want this to be orthogonal to $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ so

In general, By DeMoivre's Theorem,
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{cis} n \theta &= (\operatorname{cis} \theta)^n \\
&= (\cos \theta + i \sin \theta)^n \\
&= \cos^n \theta + \binom{n}{1} i \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta - \binom{n}{3} i \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dotsb.
\end{align*}Matching real and imaginary parts, we get
\begin{align*}
\cos n \theta &= \cos^n \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \binom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb, \\
\sin n \theta &= \binom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \binom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\tan n \theta &= \frac{\sin n \theta}{\cos n \theta} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb}{\cos^n \theta - \dbinom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \tan \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \tan^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \tan^5 \theta - \dotsb}{1 - \dbinom{n}{2} \tan^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \tan^4 \theta - \dotsb}.
\end{align*}Taking $n = 9,$ we get
\[\tan 9 \theta = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Note that for $\theta = 5^\circ,$ $25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $165^\circ,$ $\tan 9 \theta = \tan 45^\circ = 1.$  Thus,
\[1 = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Let $t = \tan \theta,$ so
\[1 = \frac{9t - 84t^3 + 126t^5 - 36t^7 + t^9}{1 - 36t^2 + 126t^4 - 84t^6 + 9t^8}.\]Thus, $\tan 5^\circ,$ $\tan 25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $\tan 165^\circ$ are the roots of
\[t^9 - 9t^8 - 36t^7 + 84t^6 + 126t^5 - 126t^4 - 84t^3 + 36t^2 + 9t - 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{9}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Multiplying both sides by $zw(z + w),$ we get
\[zw = (z + w)^2,\]which simplifies to $w^2 + zw + z^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot z,\]so the solutions are $w = z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$ and $w = z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ,$ which means that $P$ is an equilateral triangle.

[asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(2 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = dir(20);
B = dir(20 + 120);
C = dir(20 + 240);

draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));

dot("$z$", A, A);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$", B, B);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ$", C, SW);
[/asy]

The side length of the equilateral triangle is
\[\left| z - \frac{-1 + i \sqrt{3}}{2} z \right| = \left| \frac{3 - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \right| |z| = \sqrt{3} \cdot 2,\]so the area of the equilateral triangle is
\[\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cdot (2 \sqrt{3})^2 = \boxed{3 \sqrt{3}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $|z|=2,$ there exists an angle $\theta$ such that $z=2e^{i\theta}.$ Let $w=re^{i\alpha},$ so
\[\frac{1}{2e^{i\theta}+re^{i\alpha}}=\frac{1}{2e^{i\theta}}+\frac{1}{re^{i\alpha}}.\]Then $re^{i\alpha}=\frac{2}{e^{i\theta}}+\frac{2}{re^{i\alpha}e^{i\theta}}.$ Writing $w$ in terms of $\theta$ and $r,$ we see that $\alpha$ is completely determined by $\theta$ and $r.$ Therefore, we can parameterize the locus of $w$ as
\[w(s)=re^{i\alpha(\theta,s)},\]where $s$ is a real parameter and $\alpha(\theta,s)$ is some function.

Since $\alpha=\alpha(\theta,s)$ is completely determined by $\theta$ and $r,$ we have that
\begin{align*}
w(s) &= re^{i\alpha(\theta,s)} \\
&= re^{i\alpha(\theta,1)} \cdot e^{i[\alpha(\theta,s) - \alpha(\theta,1)]} \\
&= w(1) \cdot e^{i[\alpha(\theta,s) - \alpha(\theta,1)]}.
\end{align*}In particular, the points $w(0)$ and $w(1)$ both lie on the circle of radius $r$ centered at 0. The distance between $w(0)$ and $w(1)$ is $|w(1)(1-e^{i[\alpha(\theta,s) - \alpha(\theta,1)]})|=|1-e^{i[\alpha(\theta,s) - \alpha(\theta,1)]}|r,$ which is maximized when $e^{i[\alpha(\theta,s) - \alpha(\theta,1)]}=-1,$ i.e. when $\alpha(\theta,s)-\alpha(\theta,1)=\pi.$ Thus, the maximum distance between $w(0)$ and $w(1)$ is $2r.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair O, Z, A, B;

O = (0,0);
Z = intersectionpoint(arc(O,2,0,180),arc(O,2,0,-180));
A = (Z + 2*dir(-60))/3;
B = (Z + 2*dir(60))/3;

draw(Circle(O,2));
draw(O--Z);
draw(O--A--B--cycle);

label("$2$", (O + Z)/2, N);
label("$z$", Z/2, E);
label("$w(0)$", A, S);
label("$w(1)$", B, dir(0));
label("$r$", (O + A)/2, W);
label("$r$", (O + B)/2, NW);
[/asy]

Therefore, $P$ is the equilateral triangle with side length $2r.$ Equating real and imaginary parts gives
\[1 = \frac{z+w}{zw}, \quad 0 = \frac{1}{z}+\frac{1}{w} - \frac{1}{zw}.\]Then $z+w=zw$ and $\frac{1}{w}=\frac{zw-1}{z},$ so
\[w^2 - zw + 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, the product of the roots is 1, so if $w$ is a root, then $\frac{1}{w}$ is also a root. Then we see that
\[P = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}(2r)^2 = \boxed{2\sqrt{3}r^2}.\]

In cylindrical coordinates, $z$ simply denotes the $z$-coordinate of a point.  Thus, for a fixed $z$-coordinate $c,$ all the points lie on a plane that is parallel to the $xy$-plane.  The answer is $\boxed{\text{(C)}}.$

[asy]
import three;
import solids;

size(200);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);
currentlight = (1,0,1);
real theta = 120;

draw((-2,0,0)--(2,0,0));
draw((0,-2,0)--(0,2,0));
draw(surface((1,1,0.5)--(1,-1,0.5)--(-1,-1,0.5)--(-1,1,0.5)--cycle),gray(0.99));
draw((0,0,-2)--(0,0,0.2));
draw((0,0,0.5)--(0,0,2));

label("$x$", (2,0,0), SW);
label("$y$", (0,2,0), E);
label("$z$", (0,0,2), N);
label("$z = c$", (-1,1,0.5), E);
[/asy]

The normal vector to the plane will be orthogonal to both
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}\]and
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix}.\]Their cross product is
\[\begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} (b - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(b - 1) \end{pmatrix}.\]By scaling, we can take $\begin{pmatrix} 1/(1 - a) \\ 1/(1 - b) \\ 1/(1 - c) \end{pmatrix}$ as the normal vector.  Since the plane passes through $(0,0,0),$ the equation of the plane is
\[\frac{x}{1 - a} + \frac{y}{1 - b} + \frac{z}{1 - c} = 0.\]Since the plane passes through $(a,1,1),$
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 0.\]Adding 1 to both sides, we get
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + 1 + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 1,\]so
\[\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = \boxed{1}.\]

Let $\mathbf{u} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.$  Then the plane containing $(0,0,0),$ $\mathbf{u},$ and $\mathbf{v}$ is given by the equation
\[\begin{pmatrix} x - 0 \\ y - 0 \\ z - 0 \end{pmatrix} = s \begin{pmatrix} a - 0 \\ 1 - 0 \\ 1 - 0 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 1 - 0 \\ b - 0 \\ 1 - 0 \end{pmatrix},\]or
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = s \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]We can check that $(1,1,c)$ lies on this plane if $s + t = 1 - c,$ so $c = 1 - s - t.$  Therefore,
\[\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 - s \\ 1 - t \\ s + t - 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]If $a = b = c = 2,$ then the points $(0,0,0),$ $(2,1,1),$ $(1,2,1),$ and $(1,1,2)$ will satisfy the given conditions.  In this case, $s = t = 1,$ so
\[\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = -\frac{1}{3}.\]From the equation of the plane, we see that
\[\frac{x}{a} + \frac{y}{1} + \frac{z}{1} = 1.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} &= \frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{a + b - 1} \\
&= \frac{b - 1}{(b - 1)(a - 1)} + \frac{a - 1}{(b - 1)(a - 1)} + \frac{1}{(b - 1)(a - 1)} \\
&= \frac{a + b - 1}{(a - 1)(b - 1)} \\
&= \frac{c}{(a - 1)(b - 1)}.
\end{align*}Since $(0,0,0),$ $(a,1,1),$ and $(1,b,1)$ are collinear,
\[\frac{a}{0 - 1} = \frac{1 - b}{a - 1},\]so $a - b = ab - a - b + 1,$ or $(a - 1)(b - 1) = 2.$  Hence,
\[\frac{c}{(a - 1)(b - 1)} = \frac{2 - a - b + ab}{2}.\]Thus, the answer is $\boxed{1/3}.$

\[

= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = 7,$ so this is one possible value of $x.$

Subtracting the third column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = -2,$ so this is another possible value of $x.$

Expanding the last determinant along the first column, we get
\begin{align*}
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= -(x + 2) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} + (x^3 + 8) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 7 & -2 \end{vmatrix} \\
&= -9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0.
\end{align*}Since we know that $x = 7$ are $x = -2$ are two solutions, we can take out a factor of $x - 7$ and $x + 2$, to get
\[-9(x - 7)(x + 2)(x + 5) = 0.\]Hence, the possible values of $x$ are $\boxed{7, -2, \text{ and } -5}.$

Expanding the determinant along the first row gives us
\[\begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 & 1 \\ x & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 1 \begin{vmatrix} 7 & -2 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
- 1 \begin{vmatrix} x & -2 \\ x^3 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
+ 1 \begin{vmatrix} x & 7 \\ x^3 & 343 \end{vmatrix}.\]We can compute the missing determinants as follows:
\begin{align*}
\begin{vmatrix} 7 & -2 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= (7)(-8) - (343)(-2) = 686, \\
\begin{vmatrix} x & -2 \\ x^3 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= (x)(-8) - (x^3)(-2) = -2x^3 + 8x, \\
\begin{vmatrix} x & 7 \\ x^3 & 343 \end{vmatrix} &= (x)(343) - (7)(x^3) = 343x - 7x^3.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\[\begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 & 1 \\ x & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 686 - (-2x^3 + 8x) + (343x - 7x^3)
= 9x^3 - 335x + 688.\]We want to solve $9x^3 - 335x + 688 = 0.$  By the Rational Root Theorem, any rational root must divide 688, or $2^4 \cdot 43.$

We readily find that $x = 4$ is a root.  Synthetic division gives us
\[
\begin{array}{c|ccc}
4 & 9 & 0 & -335 \\ \hline
& & 36 & 144 \\ 
& 9 & 36 & -191 \\
\end{array}
\]Since $9x^3 - 335x + 688$ is a cubic, there can be at most two real roots.  The quadratic factor has discriminant
\[36^2 - 4(9)(-191) = 12012,\]which is positive, so both of the roots of $9x^3 - 335x + 688 = 0$ are real.  Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{4}.$

We have that
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|}.\]Let $d = \|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|.$  Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{c}$ are mutually orthogonal, $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} = 0.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = d^2.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \mathbf{c} + 2(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \\
&= d^2 + d^2 + d^2 \\
&= 3d^2.
\end{align*}Hence, $\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = d \sqrt{3},$ so
\[\cos \theta = \frac{d^2}{d \cdot d \sqrt{3}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}.\]

We have that
\begin{align*}
\|(\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) - \mathbf{a}\|^2 &= ((\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})) \\
&= \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + 2 \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2.
\end{align*}Also,
\[\|(\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) - \mathbf{a}\|^2 = \|(\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})\|^2 + 2 (\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})) + \|\mathbf{a}\|^2.\]Since $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = 0,$ the middle term simplifies to $2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 = 2.$  Hence,
\[\|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + 2 \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2 = \|\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 + 2 (\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})) + \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 = 5.\]Also,
\[\|\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = \sqrt{2} \|\mathbf{b}\| = \sqrt{2} \|\mathbf{c}\|,\]so $2 \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} = \|\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 - (\|\mathbf{b}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{c}\|^2) = 2.$

Therefore,
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) + 2}{2 \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|} = \frac{1}{2 \sqrt{5}} (2 + 2) = \boxed{\frac{2 \sqrt{5}}{5}}.\]

Since $\angle A,$ $\angle B,$ $\angle C$ form an arithmetic sequence, $2 \angle B = \angle A + \angle C.$  Then
\[3 \angle B = \angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ,\]which means $\angle B = 60^\circ,$ and $\angle A + \angle C = 120^\circ.$

Let $h$ be the altitude from $B.$

[asy]
unitsize (1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = (0,0);
B = 5*dir(40);
C = (5,0);
D = (B.x,0);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, N);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$h$", (B + D)/2, E);
[/asy]

Then
\[h = AB - BC = \frac{h}{\sin A} - \frac{h}{\sin C},\]so $1 = \frac{1}{\sin A} - \frac{1}{\sin C}.$  Hence,
\[\sin C - \sin A = \sin A \sin C.\]We can write this as
\[2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} \cos \frac{A + C}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) - \cos (A + C)}{2}.\]Since $A + C = 120^\circ,$
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) + \frac{1}{2}}{2} = \frac{\cos (C - A) + \frac{1}{2}}{2}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= 2 \cos (C - A) + 1 \\
&= 2 \left( 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} \right) + 1 \\
&= 3 - 4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2},
\end{align*}so
\[4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} + 4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 3 = 0.\]This factors as
\[\left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 1 \right) \left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} + 3 \right) = 0.\]Thus, the only possible value of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $x$ be the common difference, so $\angle B = \angle A + x$ and $\angle C = \angle A + 2x.$  Let $h$ be the length of the altitude from $B$ to $\overline{AC}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = (-1,0);
B = (0,1);
C = (1,0);
D = foot(B,A,C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, N);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
[/asy]

Then
\begin{align*}
AB - BC &= BD \sin \angle B - BD \sin \angle C \\
&= BD (\sin \angle B - \sin \angle C) \\
&= 2 BD \sin \frac{\angle B - \angle C}{2} \\
&= 2 BD \sin x.
\end{align*}

Let $K$ be the area of triangle $ABC.$  Then
\[h \cdot AC = 2K = AB \cdot BC \sin A = BD^2 \sin \angle A = BD^2 \sin (\angle A + 3x).\]Hence,
\[h \cdot AC = BD^2 \sin (\angle A + 3x) = BD^2 \left( \sin \angle A \cos 3x + \cos \angle A \sin 3x \right).\]Since $BD \sin \angle A = h,$ this becomes
\[h AC = h BD \left( \cos 3x \tan \angle A + \sin 3x \right).\]Since $AC = BD \sin \angle B,$ this becomes
\[h^2 \cot \angle B = h BD \left( \cos 3x \tan \angle A + \sin 3x \right).\]Substituting $h = AB - BC = BD \sin A - BD \sin B = BD \sin \angle A - BD \sin \angle A \cos x = BD \sin \angle A \sin^2 \frac{x}{2},$ we get
\[BD^2 \sin \angle B \cos^2 \frac{x}{2} = BD^2 \sin \angle A \sin^2 \frac{x}{2} \left( \cos 3x \tan \angle A + \sin 3x \right).\]Then
\[\frac{\sin \angle B}{\sin \angle A} \cos^2 \frac{x}{2} = \sin^2 \frac{x}{2} (\cos 3x \tan \angle A + \sin 3x).\]Note that $\cos 3x = \cos (\angle C - \angle A) = \cos (2x) = 2 \cos^2 x - 1.$  Hence,
\[\cos 3x \tan \angle A + \sin 3x = \frac{\cos 3x \sin \angle A}{\cos \angle A} + \frac{3 \sin x - 4 \sin^3 x}{4 \cos^3 x - 3 \cos x}.\]Let $t = \tan \frac{\angle A}{2}.$  Then $t^2 = \frac{1 - \cos \angle A}{1 + \cos \angle A} = \frac{1 - \cos x}{1 + 2 \cos x},$ so $\cos x = \frac{1 - t^2}{1 + t^2}.$  Also,
\[\sin x = \sqrt{1 - \cos^2 x} = \frac{2t}{1 + t^2}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\cos 3x \tan \angle A + \sin 3x &= \frac{(2 \cos^2 x - 1) \cdot \frac{2t}{1 + t^2}}{2t} + \frac{3 \cdot \frac{2t}{1 + t^2} - 4 \cdot \left( \frac{2t}{1 + t^2} \right)^3}{4 \left( \frac{1 - t^2}{1 + t^2} \right)^3 - 3 \cdot \frac{1 - t^2}{1 + t^2}} \\
&= \frac{4 (1 - t^2)^2 t + (1 + 3t^2) (1 - t^2)^3}{(1 - t^2)^3 + 12t^2 (1 - t^2)^2 + 4t^4 (1 - t^2)} \\
&= \frac{-8 t^5 + 6 t^3 + 9t}{3t^2 - 1}.
\end{align*}Note that $t = \tan \frac{\angle A}{2}$ is positive, since $0 < \angle A < 180^\circ.$  Also,
\[\frac{\sin \angle B}{\sin \angle A} \cos^2 \frac{x}{2} = \frac{\sin (x + \angle A)}{\sin \angle A} \cos^2 \frac{x}{2} = \frac{2 \cos \frac{x}{2} \sin \frac{x + \angle A}{2}}{2 \sin \frac{\angle A}{2} \cos \frac{x}{2}} \cos^2 \frac{x}{2}.\]Since $\angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ,$ $\angle A + \angle B > 90^\circ,$ so $x > 0.$  Also, $\sin \angle A,$ $\sin \angle B,$ and $\sin \angle C$ are all positive, so $0 < \angle A < \angle B < \angle C.$  Hence, $\frac{\angle C - \angle A}{2} = x,$ which is positive.

Since $\frac{\angle C - \angle A}{2} = x,$ $\angle A = \frac{\angle C - \angle A}{2} - x = -\frac{x}{2}.$  Then $\tan \frac{\angle A}{2} = -\tan \frac{x}{4},$ so
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \sin (A + x) = \sin \left( -\frac{x}{2} + x \right) = \sin \frac{3x}{2}.\]We want the possible values of $\sin \frac{3x}{2},$ subject to the condition that $x > 0.$

Let $y = \tan \frac{x}{2}.$  Then $\cos x = \frac{1 - y^2}{1 + y^2}$ and $\sin x = \frac{2y}{1 + y^2},$ so
\[\sin \frac{3x}{2} = \sin \frac{3 \tan^{-1} y}{2} = \frac{3y - y^3}{1 - 3y^2}.\]Suppose $\frac{3x}{2} = \frac{3 \tan^{-1} y}{2}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\tan \frac{3x}{2} &= \tan \frac{3 \tan^{-1} y}{2} \\
&= \frac{3y - y^3}{1 - 3y^2}.
\end{align*}To be more specific, the function $\tan \frac{3x}{2}$ takes on all values of the form $\frac{3y - y^3}{1 - 3y^2},$ as $y$ varies over all real numbers.  (When $y = \infty,$ we take $\frac{3y - y^3}{1 - 3y^2} = -\infty,$ and when $y = -\infty,$ we take $\frac{3y - y^3}{1 - 3y^2} = \infty.$)  In particular, $\sin \frac{3x}{2}$ takes on all values of the form $\frac{3y - y^3}{\sqrt{1 + 9y^2 - 6y^4}},$ as $y$ varies over all real numbers.

Thus, the possible values of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ are of the form
\[\frac{3y - y^3}{\sqrt{1 + 9y^2 - 6y^4}}\]for some positive $y.$  However, this expression is negative for some values of $y,$ so we should rule these out.

Note that
\[\frac{d}{dy} \left( \frac{3y - y^3}{\sqrt{1 + 9y^2 - 6y^4}} \right) = \frac{-2y^3 + 9y}{(1 + 9y^2 - 6y^4)^{3/2}}.\]This is positive when $0 < y < \frac{3 \sqrt{2}}{4}$ and negative when $\frac{3 \sqrt{2}}{4} < y < \infty.$  Thus, the possible values of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ are
\[\frac{3y - y^3}{\sqrt{1 + 9y^2 - 6y^4}}\]for $0 < y < \frac{3 \sqrt{2}}{4}.$

Furthermore, as $y$ varies over $(0,\infty),$ $3y - y^3$ ranges over $(-\infty,\infty),$ so the possible values of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ are $\boxed{(-\infty,\infty)}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix}$ is the normal vector, its projection is the zero vector.  Thus,
\[\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5} \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $\frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} = 0,$ $-\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c = 0,$ and $\frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} = 0.$  These reduce to
\begin{align*}
13a - 2b + 3c &= 0, \\
-a + 5b + 3c &= 0, \\
3a + 6b + 5c &= 0.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first two equations, we get $14a - 7b = 0,$ so $b = 2a.$  Then
\[-a + 10a + 3c = 0,\]so $c = -3a.$  Hence,
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 2a \\ -3a \end{pmatrix} = a \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix},\]so the vector we seek is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}.$

Let $\mathbf{n}$ be the normal vector of plane $P.$  Then
\[\begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{n} = \mathbf{n}.\]Then
\[\mathbf{0} = \left( \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} - \mathbf{I} \right) \mathbf{n} = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{-1}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{-2}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{-9}{14} \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{n}.\]Then by Cramer's rule,
\begin{align*}
n_1 &= \frac{\begin{vmatrix} \frac{1}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ \frac{-2}{7} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{7} & \frac{3}{14} & \frac{-9}{14} \end{vmatrix}}{\begin{vmatrix} \frac{-1}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{-2}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{-9}{14} \end{vmatrix}}} = 1, \\
n_2 &= \frac{\begin{vmatrix} \frac{-1}{14} & \frac{1}{14} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{2}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{14} & \frac{-9}{14} \end{vmatrix}}{\begin{vmatrix} \frac{-1}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{-2}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{-9}{14} \end{vmatrix}}} = -1, \\
n_3 &= \frac{\begin{vmatrix} \frac{-1}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{1}{7} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{-2}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \end{vmatrix}}{\begin{vmatrix} \frac{-1}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{-2}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{-9}{14} \end{vmatrix}}} = 2.
\end{align*}Hence, the normal vector is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}}.$
